	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Doctor (title)</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the title. For professionals frequently referred to as "doctor", see <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">Physician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surgeon" title="Surgeon">Surgeon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dentist" title="Dentist">Dentist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">Veterinarian</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Doctor_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Doctor (disambiguation)">Doctor (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Dr." redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/DR_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="DR (disambiguation)">DR (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p><p><b>Doctor</b> is an academic title that originates from the Latin word of the same spelling and meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The word is originally an <a href="/wiki/Agent_(grammar)" title="Agent (grammar)">agentive</a> noun of the Latin verb <i lang="la" title="Latin language text">docēre</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[dɔˈkeːrɛ]</a></span> 'to teach'. It has been used as an academic title in Europe since the 13th century, when the first Doctorates were awarded at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>. Having become established in European <a href="/wiki/Universities" class="mw-redirect" title="Universities">universities</a>, this usage spread around the world. Contracted "Dr" or "Dr.", it is used as a designation for a person who has obtained a <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">Doctorate</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">PhD</a>). In many parts of the world it is also used by medical practitioners, regardless of whether or not they hold a doctoral-level degree.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The doctorate (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">doceō</i>, <small><a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a>&#160;</small>&#39;I teach&#39;) appeared in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a> as a license to teach (Latin: <i lang="la">licentia docendi</i>) at a <a href="/wiki/Medieval_university" title="Medieval university">medieval university</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Doctor,_doctoratus_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Doctor,_doctoratus-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Its roots can be traced to the <a href="/wiki/Early_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Early church">early church</a> when the term "doctor" referred to the <a href="/wiki/Apostles" title="Apostles">Apostles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_fathers" class="mw-redirect" title="Church fathers">church fathers</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> authorities who taught and interpreted the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Doctor,_doctoratus_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Doctor,_doctoratus-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The right to grant a <i>licentia docendi</i> was originally reserved to the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic church">church</a> which required the applicant to pass a test, take an <a href="/wiki/Oath_of_allegiance" title="Oath of allegiance">Oath of allegiance</a> and pay a fee. The <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_the_Lateran" title="Third Council of the Lateran">Third Council of the Lateran</a> of 1179 guaranteed the access – now largely free of charge – of all able applicants, who were, however, still tested for aptitude by the ecclesiastic scholastic.<sup id="cite_ref-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Licentia_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Licentia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This right remained a bone of contention between the church authorities and the slowly emancipating universities, but was granted by the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">pope</a> to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a> in 1213 where it became a universal license to teach (<i>licentia ubiquie docendi</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Licentia_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Licentia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> However, while the licentia continued to hold a higher prestige than the bachelor's degree (<i>Baccalaureus</i>), it was ultimately reduced to an intermediate step to the Magister and doctorate, both of which now became the exclusive qualification for teaching.<sup id="cite_ref-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Licentia_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lexikon_des_Mittelalters:_Licentia-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest doctoral degrees (theology, law, and medicine) reflected the historical separation of all university study into these three fields. Over time the D.D. has gradually become less common and studies outside theology, law, and medicine have become more common (such studies were then called "philosophy", but are now classified as sciences and humanities – however this usage survives in the degree of Doctor of Philosophy).
</p><p>The Ph.D. was originally a <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">degree</a> granted by a <a href="/wiki/University" title="University">university</a> to learned individuals who had achieved the approval of their peers and who had demonstrated a long and productive career in the field of philosophy (in the broad sense of the term, meaning the pursuit of knowledge). The appellation of "Doctor" (from Latin: teacher) was usually awarded only when the individual was in middle age. It indicated a life dedicated to learning, knowledge, and the spread of knowledge. The Ph.D. entered widespread use in the 19th century at <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin" title="Humboldt University of Berlin">Friedrich Wilhelm University</a> in Berlin as a degree to be granted to someone who had undertaken original research in the sciences or humanities. Prior to the formal degree, the contemporary doctorate (PhD), arguably, arose in Leipzig as a successor to the Master's (MA) degree in 1652 (Dr. habil).<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some European countries, such as Italy and Portugal, Doctor became a title given to all or most degree holders, not just those with doctorates.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> 
As a result, the title is now used by many professionals in these countries, including those such as lawyers who are not normally granted the title elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The title is also used for lawyers in South America, where they have traditionally earned doctoral degrees,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> as well as in the former Portuguese territory of <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> in China.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_in_English-speaking_countries">Development in English-speaking countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Development in English-speaking countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The primary meaning of <i>Doctor</i> in English has historically been with reference to the holder of a doctoral degree.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson1_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> These particularly referred to the ancient faculties of divinity, law and medicine, sometimes with the addition of music, which were the only doctoral degrees offered until the 19th century. During the 19th century, Ph.D.s became increasingly common in Britain, although to obtain the degree it was necessary to travel to continental Europe or (from 1861) to the United States, as the degree was not awarded in the UK until 1917.
</p><p>However, the title, not being protected by law, was adopted by <a href="/wiki/Quackery" title="Quackery">quacks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> As a result, by the mid 19th century, it was normal in the UK to omit the title "Dr" when addressing letters to those holding doctoral degrees, and instead write the abbreviated form of the degree after the name, e.g. "The Reverend Robert Phelps, D.D.", "Thomas Elliotson, Esq. M.D.", or "John Lindsey, Esq. Ph.D.", in order to avoid classing academic doctors "with the village apothecary and the farrier" and various "quacks in literature, science, or art".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In the US it similarly became customary to use post-nominals rather than the title of Doctor when addressing letters.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> All those with doctoral degrees continued to use the title professionally and socially.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite being historically associated with doctorates in law, the title of <i>doctor</i> for lawyers has not customarily been used in English-speaking countries, where lawyers were traditionally not required to have a university degree and were trained by other lawyers by apprenticeship or in the Inns of Court.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The exception being those areas where, up to the 19th century, civil law rather than common law was the governing tradition, including <a href="/wiki/Admiralty_law" title="Admiralty law">admiralty law</a>, probate and ecclesiastical law: such cases were heard in the <a href="/wiki/Doctor%27s_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor&#39;s Commons">Doctor's Commons</a>, and argued by advocates who held degrees either of doctor of civil law at Oxford or doctor of law at Cambridge. As such, lawyers practicing common law in England were not doctoral candidates and had not earned a doctorate. When university degrees became more common for those wanting to qualify as a lawyer in England, the degree awarded was the <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Laws" title="Bachelor of Laws">Bachelor of Laws</a> (LL.B.). Similarly in the US, even though degrees became standard for lawyers much earlier, the degree was again the LL.B., only becoming the <a href="/wiki/Juris_Doctor" title="Juris Doctor">Juris Doctor</a> generally in the latter half of the 20th century.
</p><p>In many English-speaking countries, it is common to refer to physicians by the title of doctor, even when they do not hold a doctoral level qualification. The word <i>Doctor</i> has long had a secondary meaning in English of <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a>, e.g. in <a href="/wiki/Johnson%27s_Dictionary" class="mw-redirect" title="Johnson&#39;s Dictionary">Johnson's Dictionary</a>, which quotes its use with this meaning by <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson1_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In the US, the medical societies established the proprietary medical colleges in the 19th century to award their own MDs,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> but in the UK and the British Empire, where degree granting was strictly controlled, this was not an option. The usage of the title to refer to medical practitioners, even when they didn't hold doctoral degrees, was common by the mid 18th century.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> However, the first official recognition of Doctor being applied as a title to medical practitioners regardless of whether they held a doctoral degree was in 1838, when the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Physicians" title="Royal College of Physicians">Royal College of Physicians</a> resolved that it would "regard in the same light, and address by the same appellation, all who have obtained its diploma, whether they have graduated elsewhere or not."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RCPHistory_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RCPHistory-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Medical_Act_1858" title="Medical Act 1858">Medical Act 1858</a> made it illegal for anyone not qualified in medicine to use a title that implied they were. This led to prosecutions of people making unauthorised use of the title "Dr".<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> However, it also called into question the use of the title by licentiates of the Colleges of Physicians – all of whom were, under the new act, allowed to practice throughout the UK. In 1859, the London College reversed its earlier decision, resolving "That the title of Doctor shall not be given in any official document issued from this College to any person who is not possessed of the Degree of Doctor of Medicine".<sup id="cite_ref-RCPHistory_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RCPHistory-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> This was followed up in 1860 by new bylaws that stated "No Fellow, Member, or Licentiate of the College shall assume the title of Doctor of Medicine, or use any other name, title, designation or distinction implying that he is a Graduate in Medicine of an University, unless he be a Graduate in Medicine of an University".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In Ireland, the question of whether the license of the  <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Physicians_of_Ireland" title="Royal College of Physicians of Ireland">Royal College of Physicians of Ireland</a> granted the title of Doctor of Medicine led to a court case in 1861, with the conclusion that it did not.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="British Medical Journal">British Medical Journal</a></i> (<i>BMJ</i>) observed, however, that anyone wanting the right to the title of "Doctor" could gain it "with a five-shilling degree of Doctor of Philosophy" from abroad, or could simply assume the title, as only "Doctor of Medicine" was actually protected.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Debate continued as to the use of "Doctor" as a courtesy title by those who did not use it by right as holders of doctoral degrees, with the <i>BMJ</i> noting in 1876 that "We have again a sort of flood of letters for and against the use of the title of Doctor by physicians" and in 1882 that "There is not any other subject which appears to excite so wide spread an interest as this".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In February 1876, a report recommended that the Royal College of Physicians should use the courtesy title of Doctor for all fellows and members, but this was rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Then in April of the same year, the College amended its bylaws to forbid any fellow, member, extra-licentiate or licentiate from using the title of Doctor unless they had a doctorate in medicine from a recognised university – closing the loophole the <i>BMJ</i> had identified.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It was not until the early 20th century that this was reversed. In 1905 the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Surgeons" title="Royal College of Surgeons">Royal College of Surgeons</a> passed a motion instructing their council "to take the necessary steps in conjunction with the Royal College of Physicians to ensure that all persons who pass the Conjoint examination shall be legally entitled to call themselves Doctors". The council of the surgeons' College felt it to be impractical to secure the legal right to the title as this would mean gaining the right to award M.D.s, but noted that the title had been used by the public to refer to medics for generations and was used without any legal right by Bachelors of Medicine – the only obstacle to licentiates of both colleges doing the same was the prohibition in the physicians' bylaws. On this occasion the College of Physicians refused to act, but they did finally relent in 1912, removing the clause forbidding the assumption of the title of Doctor.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This was described in the American press as "the British apostles of red-tape have been forced to bow to the popular will".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Regulation of the medical profession also took place in the United States in the latter half of the 19th century, preventing quacks from using the title of Doctor.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> However, medical usage of the title was far from exclusive, with it being acknowledged that other doctorate holders could use the title and that dentists and veterinarians frequently did.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Etiquette of To-day</i>, published in 1913, recommended addressing letters to physicians "(full name), M.D." and those to other people holding doctorates "Dr. (full name)", although both were "Dr." in the salutation and only physicians were explicitly said to include their title on their <a href="/wiki/Visiting_card" title="Visiting card">visiting card</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> By the 1920s there were a great variety of doctorates in the US, many of them taking entrants directly from high school, and ranging from the Doctor of Chiropractic (DC), which (at the time) required only two or three years of college-level education,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> up to the PhD. All doctoral degree holders, with the exception of the JD, were customarily addressed as "Doctor", but the title was also regularly used, without doctoral degrees, by pharmacists, ministers of religion, teachers and chiropodists, and sometimes by other professions such as beauty practitioners, patent medicine manufacturers, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 1940s, the widespread usage of the title in the US was under threat. A 1944 article claimed that "the Ph.D. has immediate and far-reaching value of  social as well as economic nature" due to America's "national fondness for the tinsel of titles", but went on to note that some universities were moving away from using the title, concluding that "it is ungracious in most environments not to render unto the Doctor of Philosophy his 'Doctor' title".<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The same writer noted in a letter to the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Higher_Education" class="mw-redirect" title="Journal of Higher Education">Journal of Higher Education</a></i> in 1948 that <a href="/wiki/Alfred_University" title="Alfred University">Alfred University</a> had banned the use of the title for faculty (while retaining it for the president and deans) "in a strange move  professedly designed to uphold and promote 'democracy' and 'Americanism<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> However, it was noted in 1959 that professors with PhDs were now generally addressed as "Doctor", with the title of "Professor" sometimes being substituted for those without doctorates, leading to a decline in the perceived value of that title.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
In the 1960s the inconsistent usage at American universities and colleges was mentioned in the <i>New York Times Book Review</i> and the editor of <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> noted that: "In some universities, administrators call all Ph.D.'s 'Mister,' but students and colleagues call them 'Doctor.' Often, but not always, Ph.D.'s are 'Misters' socially. In industry and government, both socially and professionally, they are 'Doctors,' as they are also in the pages of the <i>New Yorker</i>, <i>Time</i>, the <i>Saturday Review</i>, and the New York <i>Times</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 1965, the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Women_Voters" title="League of Women Voters">League of Women Voters</a> designated medical doctors "Dr." and PhDs "Mr." at a hustings in Princeton, leading to a letter of protest in <i>Science</i>; it was reported that the League believed PhDs would be embarrassed by the title, and that etiquette writers differed in whether PhDs used the title.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 1970, reverse snobbism in the face of the rising number of "discount doctorates" was linked to  professors at prestigious universities wanting to be called "mister".<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 1960s the rising number of American law schools awarding <a href="/wiki/Juris_Doctor" title="Juris Doctor">Juris Doctor</a> (JD) degrees led to debate over whether lawyers could ethically use the title "Doctor". Initial informal ethics opinions, based on the Canons of Professional Ethics then in force, came down against this.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> These were then reinforced with a full ethics opinion that maintained the ban on using the title in legal practice as a form of self-laudation (except when dealing with countries where the use of "Doctor" by lawyers was standard practice), but allowed the use of the title in academia "if the school of graduation thinks of the J.D. degree as a doctor's degree".<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> These opinions led to further debate.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of the new Code of Professional Responsibility in 1969 seemed to settle the question &#8211; in states where this was adopted &#8211; in favour of allowing the use of the title.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> There was some dispute over whether only the PhD-level <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Juridical_Science" title="Doctor of Juridical Science">Doctor of Juridical Science</a> should properly be seen as granting the title,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> but ethics opinions made it clear that the new Code allowed JD-holders to be called "Doctor", while reaffirming that the older Canons did not.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> As not all state bars adopted the new Code, and some omitted the clause permitting the use of the title, confusion over whether lawyers could ethically use the title "Doctor" continued.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of further <a href="/wiki/Professional_doctorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Professional doctorate">professional doctorates</a> in the US at <a href="/wiki/ISCED" class="mw-redirect" title="ISCED">ISCED</a> level 7, the same as the MD and JD, has led to continuing debate about the use of the title by holders of such degrees, particularly in medical contexts.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2018, a decision by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Globe_and_Mail" title="The Globe and Mail">The Globe and Mail</a></i> newspaper in Canada to update its style guide so as to restrict the use of the title Doctor to medics led to a backlash on <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>, particularly by women with PhDs, using the #ImmodestWomen <a href="/wiki/Hashtag" title="Hashtag">hashtag</a>. This was widely reported on internationally and led to <i>The Globe and Mail</i> reverting to its earlier style of using Doctor for both physicians and PhD holders.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The Canadian <a href="/wiki/University_of_Calgary" title="University of Calgary">University of Calgary</a> also announced that it would adopt the use of Doctor for those with doctoral degrees, breaking with the style recommended by the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Press" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadian Press">Canadian Press</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctor_as_a_noun">Doctor as a noun</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Doctor as a noun">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Throughout much of the academic world, the term <i>Doctor</i> refers to someone who has earned a doctoral degree (highest degree) from a university.<sup id="cite_ref-OED_Doctor_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED_Doctor-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> This is normally the <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Doctor of Philosophy</a>, abbreviated PhD (sometimes Ph.D. in North America) from the Latin <i>Philosophiae Doctor</i> or DPhil from its English name,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> or equivalent research doctorates at level 8 of the <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Classification_of_Education" title="International Standard Classification of Education">International Standard Classification of Education</a> 2011 classifications (ISCED 2011) or level 6 of the ISCED 1997 classifications. Beyond academia (but specifically in the Anglo-Saxon world), <i>Doctor</i> as a noun normally refers to a medical practitioner,<sup id="cite_ref-OED_Doctor_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED_Doctor-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> who would usually hold a qualification at level 7 of ISCED 2011/level 5 of ISCED 1997 such as the British <a href="/wiki/MBBS" class="mw-redirect" title="MBBS">MBBS</a> or the American <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">MD</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_of_address">Forms of address</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Forms of address">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When addressing several people, each of whom holds a doctoral title, one may use the plural contraction "Drs" (or "Drs." in <a href="/wiki/American_and_British_English_spelling_differences" title="American and British English spelling differences">American English</a>) – or in some languages (for example, German) "Dres." (from the Latin <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/doctores#Latin" class="extiw" title="wikt:doctores">doctores</a>) may be used – for example, instead of Dr. Miller and Dr. Rubinstein: Drs. Miller and Rubinstein. When referring to relatives with the same surname the form "The Doctors Smith" can be used. The abbreviation Drs. can also refer to <a href="/wiki/Doctorandus" title="Doctorandus">doctorandus</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> academic title.
</p><p>In English, Dr is not usually combined with other titles, except for <a href="/wiki/The_Reverend" title="The Reverend">The Reverend</a> in "The Revd Dr" before the surname of a minister of religion, e.g., "The Revd Dr Smith" or "The Revd John Smith, DD", and similarly "Rabbi Dr". In <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_English" title="Caribbean English">Caribbean English</a>, the usage "Dr. the Honourable" is common for politicians holding doctoral degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Usage in many other languages is similar to English but some, notably German, allow for the stacking of titles.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Usage_by_medical_practitioners_in_the_UK_and_culturally-related_countries">Usage by medical practitioners in the UK and culturally-related countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Usage by medical practitioners in the UK and culturally-related countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, New Zealand, <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and other countries whose cultures were recently linked to the UK, the title <i>Dr</i> is generally used both for those who hold doctoral degrees and for <a href="/wiki/Registered_medical_practitioner" class="mw-redirect" title="Registered medical practitioner">registered medical practitioners</a>. History has dictated the use of the courtesy title <i>Dr</i> by physicians and general practitioners.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Surgeons" class="mw-redirect" title="Surgeons">surgeons</a> do not use the title of <i>Dr</i> and, due to the origins of surgery with the <a href="/wiki/Barber_surgeon" title="Barber surgeon">barber surgeons</a>, instead use <i><a href="/wiki/Mister_(Mr.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mister (Mr.)">Mr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mrs." title="Mrs.">Mrs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ms." title="Ms.">Ms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miss" title="Miss">Miss</a></i>, etc. This custom applies to surgeons of any grade who have passed the appropriate exams and is not the exclusive province of consultant-level surgeons. In recent times, other surgically orientated specialists, such as <a href="/wiki/Gynaecologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Gynaecologist">gynaecologists</a>, have also adopted these prefixes. A surgeon who is also a professor is usually known as "Professor" and, similarly, a surgeon who has been ennobled, knighted, created a baronet or appointed a dame uses the corresponding title (Lord, Sir, Dame). <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">Physicians</a>, on the other hand, when they pass their "<a href="/wiki/Membership_of_the_Royal_College_of_Physicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Membership of the Royal College of Physicians">MRCP(UK)</a>" examinations, or equivalent, do not drop <i>Dr</i> but retain it, even as consultants. The status and rank of consultant surgeons, addressed as "Mister", and consultant physicians, addressed  as "Doctor", is equivalent.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide_usage">Worldwide usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Worldwide usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_doctoral_degrees_awarded_by_country" title="List of doctoral degrees awarded by country">List of doctoral degrees awarded by country</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doctorate#Practice_by_country" title="Doctorate">Doctorate §&#160;Practice by country</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy#Degrees_around_the_globe" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Doctor of Philosophy §&#160;Degrees around the globe</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bangladesh">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, use of the title of Doctor (Dr.) is permitted for Ph.D. degree holders and registered medical practitioners. According to an amendment, universities are allowed to approve or reject any subject proposed for a thesis by a candidate for the degree of "Doctor" in any subject.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (December 2016)">failed verification</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> For registered medical practitioners, only MBBS and BDS degree holders are allowed to use the title "Dr." and be called "Medical Doctors".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Registered veterinary practitioners may use the title "Dr." after earning the "Doctor of Veterinary Medicine" (DVM) degree. However, Registered Homeopath practitioners also use the title "Dr." even though, according to Homeopathic Practitioners Ordinance 1983, they are only permitted to use "Homeopath".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Currently, Physiotherapy has no separate council and no authorized act at present permits the use of the prefix  "Dr."  for physiotherapist. According to Bangladesh Unani &amp; Ayurvedic Practitioners Ordinance 1983, practitioners of the Unani system are called  “Tabib” or “Hakim” and are strictly prohibited from using the title  "Dr."; similarly, practitioners of Ayurvedic system are called “Vaid” or “Kabiraj” and are also strictly prohibited from using  "Dr.".<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Currently, medical practitioners having MBBS degree or dental surgeons having BDS are legally permitted to use "Dr." prefix. Using "Dr." for other medical practitioners remains controversial.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Kong">Hong Kong</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hong Kong">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> follows British practice in calling physicians "Doctor" even though many of them hold only a degree of Bachelor of Medicine and Bachelor of Surgery (MBBS or MBChB). An attempt by their professional body to prevent <a href="/wiki/Chiropractors" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiropractors">chiropractors</a> from calling themselves "Doctor" failed in the courts, in part because it was pointed out that practicing chiropractors may hold an academic doctorate in their discipline, and it would be anomalous to prevent them using the title when holders of doctorates in non-medical disciplines faced no such restriction.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, professional practitioners in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dentistry" title="Dentistry">dentistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_medicine" title="Veterinary medicine">veterinary medicine</a> are usually addressed as doctor even though they hold bachelor's-level degrees. The legal right to use the title and to add the prefix 'Dr' to their name follows recognition by the <a href="/wiki/Medical_Council_of_India" title="Medical Council of India">Medical Council of India</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_Council_of_India" title="Veterinary Council of India">Veterinary Council of India</a>.
</p><p>A person who has been awarded a <a href="/wiki/PhD" class="mw-redirect" title="PhD">PhD</a> (or other doctoral level degree) by a university recognized by the <a href="/wiki/University_Grants_Commission_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="University Grants Commission of India">University Grants Commission of India</a>, is entitled to be addressed as doctor and to prefix the abbreviation 'Dr' to their name.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Indonesian titles "dr." is used in front of the name of medical doctor who holds a specification as general practitioner, also when the doctor already holds his specialization to ___, such as "Sp.THT" or "Spesialis Telinga, Hidung, Tenggorokan" (ENT or Ear, Nose, and Throat Specialist).
</p><p>Dr. is used in front of the name as the title "Doktor" for doctorate title, the same level as Ph.D. title.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pakistan">Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, the title of Doctor (Dr.) can be used by <a href="/wiki/Ph.D." class="mw-redirect" title="Ph.D.">Ph.D.</a> degree holders as well as medical, dental and veterinary doctors with <a href="/wiki/MBBS" class="mw-redirect" title="MBBS">MBBS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dental_degree" title="Dental degree">BDS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veterinary_education" title="Veterinary education">DVM</a> degrees respectively. Usage by <a href="/wiki/Pharmacists" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmacists">Pharmacists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physiotherapists" class="mw-redirect" title="Physiotherapists">physiotherapists</a> with <a href="/wiki/PharmD" class="mw-redirect" title="PharmD">PharmD</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_physical_therapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of physical therapy">DPT</a> degrees respectively is disputed, with the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Medical_and_Dental_Council" title="Pakistan Medical and Dental Council">Pakistan Medical and Dental Council</a> saying they should not use the title, but the <a href="/wiki/Pharmacy_Council_of_Pakistan" title="Pharmacy Council of Pakistan">Pharmacy Council of Pakistan</a> (the regulatory body for pharmacists) and the <a href="/wiki/Higher_Education_Commission_(Pakistan)" title="Higher Education Commission (Pakistan)">Higher Education Commission</a> permitting and encouraging its use.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Philippines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, titles and names of occupations usually follow <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> naming conventions which utilize gender-specific terms. "<i>Doktór</i>" is the masculine form, which retains the abbreviation <i>Dr.</i>; the feminine form is "<i>Doktóra</i>", and is abbreviated usually as "<i>Dra.</i>"; others, however, some being <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">Anglophones</a> who wish to sound modern and Westernised (or were raised in an almost exclusively English-speaking family environment), or some who advocate gender equality, would dispense with the distinction altogether. There does exist in <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">Filipino</a> an equivalent, gender-neutral term for the professional that carries the more general notion of "healer", traditional (for example, an <i><a href="/wiki/Albularyo" class="mw-redirect" title="Albularyo">albuláryo</a></i>) or otherwise: <i>manggagámot</i>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sri_Lanka">Sri Lanka</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sri Lanka">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> the title doctor "Dr." is used for both physicians (including dentists and veterinarians) and Ph.D. holders. However, when addressing in native <a href="/wiki/Sinhalese_people" title="Sinhalese people">Sinhalese</a> a physician is addressed as "Vaidya" (වෛද්ය) or "Dosthara" (දොස්තර) while a Ph.D. holder is addressed as "Aacharya" (ආචාර්ය).
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The usage of Doctor (ดอกเตอร์) or Dr (ดร.) has been borrowed from English. It can be seen as a title in academic circles and in the mass media. In contrast to other academic titles (Professor, Associate Professor and Assistant Professor), the use of Doctor as a title has not been recognized by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Institute_of_Thailand" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Institute of Thailand">Royal Institute of Thailand</a>. Therefore, this title, in theory, cannot be used officially. For example, in court of justice where strictly formal Thai language is used, Dr cannot be mentioned as a person's title.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Americas">The Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: The Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Canada lies somewhere between British and American usage of the degree and terminology of "doctor". Research doctorates – PhDs and ScDs – are entitled to use the title "doctor". Healthcare professions for which members may be called by the title of doctor are: medicine, dentistry, clinical psychology, optometry, chiropractic, veterinary medicine, podiatry and, more recently, pharmacy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In Alberta, Registered Nurses or Nurse Practitioners with an earned doctoral degree may use the title "doctor" in conjunction with professional practice.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Members of these professions generally complete a minimum of 2 years of undergraduate studies, followed by a four- to six-year professional program (considered, despite the name of the degree, to be at bachelor's-level<sup id="cite_ref-cdqf_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdqf-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>) to earn the <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">Doctor of Medicine</a> (M.D.), <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Dental_Surgery" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Dental Surgery">Doctor of Dental Surgery</a> (D.D.S.), <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Psychology" title="Doctor of Psychology">Doctor of Psychology</a> (Psy.D.), <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Optometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Optometry">Doctor of Optometry</a> (O.D.), <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Chiropractic" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Chiropractic">Doctor of Chiropractic</a> (D.C.) or <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Veterinary_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Veterinary Medicine">Doctor of Veterinary Medicine</a> (D.V.M.). In some provinces it is also possible to earn the <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Podiatric_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Podiatric Medicine">Doctor of Podiatric Medicine</a> (D.P.M) or <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Pharmacy" title="Doctor of Pharmacy">Doctor of Pharmacy</a> (Pharm.D.). In some provinces, such as Ontario, psychologists take a PhD in Psychology rather than the PsyD degree. In Ontario, only chiropractors, dentists, medical doctors, optometrists and psychologists can use the title "doctor", when offering or purporting to offer health related services, while registered naturopathic doctors (N.D.) may only use the title "doctor" in written format if they also use the phrase, "naturopathic doctor" immediately following their name. A 2006 amendment that would allow practitioners of Traditional Chinese Medicine to use the title has not, as of 1&#160;August&#160;2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, entered into force.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some confusion has recently arisen with respect to Canada's legal profession, after many Canadian law schools started offering to grant the J.D. ("Juridicae Doctor" or "Juris Doctor") degree (considered, like the medical professional degrees, to be at bachelor's level<sup id="cite_ref-cdqf_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdqf-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>) to their existing students and to their alumni - as a replacement for the degree they traditionally awarded: the <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Laws#Canada" title="Bachelor of Laws">LL.B.</a> ("Legum Baccalaureus" or "Bachelor of Laws"). The course requirements did not change, only the name. Canadian law schools started offering this option to  their graduates to make it easier to practise in the United States (where law schools have traditionally granted a J.D. rather than an LL.B.). The convention governing use of the title "Dr" by lawyers has not changed in Canada. The only lawyers who typically use "Dr" are those who have continued with their studies, in order to obtain both an <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Laws" title="Master of Laws">LL.M.</a> ("Legum Magister" or "Master of Laws") and an <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Juridical_Science" title="Doctor of Juridical Science">S.J.D.</a> ("Scientiae Juridicae Doctor" or "Doctor of the Science of the Law"). Sometimes also referred to as a "J.S.D.",<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> the S.J.D. degree is legal discipline's equivalent to a Ph.D. degree. The McGill University Faculty of Law offers a doctoral level program in law, but it grants a D.C.L. ("Doctor of Civil Law") instead.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> To save money, some Canadian law schools (including U.B.C. and Calgary) have opted to work with their respective faculties of graduate studies to offer Ph.D. degrees in law instead of founding new S.J.D. programs.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Quebec">Quebec</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Quebec">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The usage of the French <i>Docteur</i> and <i>Docteure</i>, and their abbreviated forms <i>Dr</i>, <i>Dre</i>, <i>D<sup>r</sup></i> and <i>D<sup>re</sup></i>, is controlled by the <i>Code des professions</i>. As a pre-nominal title it can be used without any further explication by physicians, veterinarians, and dentists. It can also be used prenominally, when accompanied by the name of the profession immediately after the name, by professionals who require a doctorate for their professional licence, such as psychology or chiropractic, e.g. <i>Dr X, psychologue</i> or <i>Dr Y, chiropraticien</i>. Academic doctors, where the doctorate is not required to practice, bear the title only after their name; this is not abbreviated, e.g. <i>M. Z, docteur en philosophie</i> not <i>M. Z, Ph.D.</i><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the United States, the use of the title "Doctor" is dependent upon the setting. The title is commonly used socially by physicians and those holding doctoral degrees;<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> however, there was formerly a division between <a href="/wiki/Letitia_Baldrige" title="Letitia Baldrige">Letitia Baldrige</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miss_Manners" class="mw-redirect" title="Miss Manners">Miss Manners</a> on its social usage by those who are not physicians.<sup id="cite_ref-kip_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kip-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Baldrige saw this usage as acceptable, while in contrast, Miss Manners wrote that "only people of the medical profession correctly use the title of doctor socially," but supports those who wish to use it in social contexts in the spirit of addressing people according to their wishes.<sup id="cite_ref-kip_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kip-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martin2005_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin2005-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Miss Manners has since softened her approach, noting in her <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> column that there are two approaches: "having been earned, it should be used" and "that level of education being assumed, it need not be expressly mentioned"; while she maintains that everyone should be addressed according to their wishes, she no longer states that only medical professionals use the title correctly but instead acknowledges that the title has been earned by those with Ph.D.s.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Emily_Post_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Emily Post Institute">Emily Post Institute</a> similarly advises that "Socially as well as professionally, medical doctors, dentists, and other professionals are addressed by, and introduced with, their titles. People who have earned a Ph.D. or any other academic, nonmedical doctoral degree have the choice of whether to use "Dr." both professionally and socially."<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Other advice columns have also noted that "it has become common to see someone with a Ph.D. addressed on the envelope as Dr., and as a consequence, deviation from convention has become acceptable."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The 2017 book <i>Etiquette Rules!</i> gives identical forms for addressing a "doctor of medicine (MD), dental surgery (DDS), veterinary medicine (DVM), etc.", and the holder of a Ph.D., advising in both cases the use of initials after the name for formal correspondence and Dr. before the name for social correspondence.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Although the usage of the title by Ph.D. graduates has become common, its use socially by holders of professional doctorates (other than those noted) is neither explicitly endorsed nor explicitly discouraged by writers on etiquette. Miss Manners has, however, stated that a physician who has had their license revoked should be addressed by their former preferred honorific (i.e. not according to their M.D. degree).<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>  It is unusual for those who hold honorary doctorates to use the title "Doctor".<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Publications from the office of the President of the United States of America also refer to Ph.D. holders as Dr. Soandso,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
and <a href="/wiki/Second_Lady" title="Second Lady">Second Lady</a> <a href="/wiki/Jill_Biden" title="Jill Biden">Jill Biden</a> used the style "Dr. Jill Biden" after earning her Ph.D.<sup id="cite_ref-JillBiden_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JillBiden-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> For addresses  (defined as "the conventional forms of address as determined by social and official custom"), <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> uses "Dr. (full name)" in addresses for Ph.D. holders while for physicians it uses "(full name), MD", although both are addressed as "Dr. (surname)" in the salutation (which is described as "informal").<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a> similarly use "Dr. (surname)" in salutations for people with an M.D., Ph.D. or D.D.S.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> They advise using full name followed by degrees in addressees, explicitly stating not to use the title "Dr.", although an example in the following paragraph does, in fact, use the title rather than giving degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most newspapers in the US follow the <a href="/wiki/AP_Stylebook" title="AP Stylebook">AP Stylebook</a> and reserve the title for medical doctors in their house styles;<sup id="cite_ref-JillBiden_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JillBiden-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> notable exceptions include <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, which follows the preference of the individual when referring to PhD holders (although the title is not used for those with honorary doctorates),<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
and the <a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a>, which similarly prefers "Dr." for Ph.D. holders and physicians (if this is the person's choice) while stating explicitly that the title is not used for lawyers with J.D.s or people with honorary doctorates.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Until 1989, <a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a> used the title for "practitioners of the healing arts (including chiropractors and osteopaths) but not for holders of PhDs or honorary degrees", after which it dropped its use entirely.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Some sources state that AP style allows the use of Dr. for holders of non-medical doctoral degrees as long as their speciality is given.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The expansion of professional doctorates in clinical fields in the late 20th and early 21st centuries has led to disputes between physicians and other medical professions over who can use the title in a clinical context. This has been interpreted by some as part of larger battles within medicine, such as who gets to treat patients first and who has prescribing authority.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a> calls for non-physicians (those not holding an M.D. or D.O.) who use the title "Doctor" and are in direct contact with patients to clarify that they are not physicians and to "define the nature of their doctorate degree",<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/American_Osteopathic_Association" title="American Osteopathic Association">American Osteopathic Association</a> opposes the use of the title by non-physicians  in clinical settings absolutely as (in their view) "such use deceives the public".<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Contrary to this, the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Nurses_Association" title="Emergency Nurses Association">Emergency Nurses Association</a> has adopted as a position statement that "1. Nurses are entitled to have their educational degrees recognized and acknowledged in the same manner as other professions. 2. The proper title protection and use of accurate credentials is appropriate in the clinical setting. 3. When being addressed or introduced as doctor in the clinical environment, it is responsible practice for all healthcare providers to clarify their professional role. 4. Patients, families and the general public have a right and expectation to be informed regarding the credentials of their caregivers, including the use of the title “doctor”."<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The American Medical Association launched a campaign in 2011 for states to adopt "truth in advertising" legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> As a result, many states now have laws in place that protect the title of doctor when offering medical services.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In some jurisdictions, the use of the title in health care is limited to those who have both a doctorate and are licensed to practice medicine, and there is a requirement that the field of the doctorate be disclosed.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Some other jurisdictions require the practitioner to hold a doctorate and to disclose the field, but make no stipulations as to licensing.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Professional boards may also place limits on its use by the practitioners they regulate, e.g. requiring either a Ph.D. in psychology or a Doctor of Psychology degree to use the title while practicing as a psychologist.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Some states require name badges to be worn in medical facilities giving first name, licensure status, and staff position, although these laws may not explicitly address the use of the title "Doctor".<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although lawyers in the United States do not customarily use the title, the law degree in that country is the <a href="/wiki/Juris_Doctor" title="Juris Doctor">Juris Doctor</a>, a professional doctorate.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethics_Opinion_1969-5_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethics_Opinion_1969-5-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Some J.D. holders in the United States use the title of doctor in professional situations, although ethics board decisions have varied on whether this is permissible or might mislead the public into believing the lawyer was medically qualified or had a PhD.<sup id="cite_ref-law.cornell.edu_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-law.cornell.edu-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> It is also sometimes used by J.D. holders in academic situations.<sup id="cite_ref-Profile_of_Dr._Michael_Harrington_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Profile_of_Dr._Michael_Harrington-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, <i><a href="/wiki/Mother_Jones_(magazine)" title="Mother Jones (magazine)">Mother Jones</a></i> published an article claiming that <a href="/wiki/Michele_Bachmann" title="Michele Bachmann">Michele Bachmann</a> was misrepresenting her qualifications by using the "bogus" title Dr. based on her J.D. The article was later amended to note that the use of the title by lawyers "is a (begrudgingly) accepted practice in some states and not in others", although they maintained that it was rarely used as it "suggests that you're a medical doctor or a Ph.D.—and therefore conveys a false level of expertise".<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical">Ecclesiastical</a> seminaries and entitled churches award their own doctorates in the United States, e.g. the <i>Doctor of Religious Science</i> (Dr. sc. rel.), the <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Divinity" title="Doctor of Divinity">Doctor of Divinity</a></i> (D.D.), the <i>Doctor of Biblical Studies</i> (D.B.S.) or the <i>Doctor of Metaphysics</i> (Dr. mph.). These titles are most commonly awarded to meritorious <a href="/wiki/Cleric" class="mw-redirect" title="Cleric">clerics</a> for their outstanding work or another achievement in the field of religious and biblical studies.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>American professional doctorates are not generally considered doctoral level degrees internationally, instead being classified as bachelor's or master's level. The <a href="/wiki/ISCED" class="mw-redirect" title="ISCED">ISCED</a> mapping for these degrees, developed collaboratively between the US and <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>, places them at master's level. As a result, holders of MD, JD, PharmD, etc. may not be permitted to use the title of Doctor in countries such as Germany where this is strictly controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the European Union, the title of <i>doctor</i> refers primarily to holders of post-graduate research doctorates, such as the PhD. In many European languages the term <i>doctor</i> is distinct from a medical practitioner, which can be referred to as e.g. <i>läkare</i> in Swedish, <i>Arzt</i> in German, <i>dokter</i> or <i>arts</i> in Dutch, or <i>lääkäri</i> in Finnish.
</p><p>Standardisation of degrees into the three cycles of bachelor's–master's–doctorate across the <a href="/wiki/European_Higher_Education_Area" title="European Higher Education Area">European Higher Education Area</a> (EHEA) is being carried out through the <a href="/wiki/Bologna_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Bologna process">Bologna process</a>, although not all EHEA member states have fully conformed to the 1999 <a href="/wiki/Bologna_declaration" title="Bologna declaration">Bologna declaration</a> in favour of their own historic customs. With respect to the title "doctor", the Bologna process makes no prescriptions regarding the names of degrees nor the titles they may confer. However, under the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon_Recognition_Convention" title="Lisbon Recognition Convention">Lisbon Recognition Convention</a>, recognition of a foreign degree allows "the use of an academic title, subject to the laws and regulations of the country in which recognition is sought".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> According to the <i>Explanatory report</i>, this means that: "The competent authorities of the Parties may grant the right to use the title in the exact form in which it was awarded in the Party concerned or in any other form. They may alternatively grant the right to use the corresponding title of the country in which recognition is sought."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Austria">Austria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Austria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Austria, the degree "Doktor" is granted to holders of research degrees (Dr. techn., Dr. phil., Dr. rer. nat., etc.).<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, the title of "Doktor" is granted to physicians (Dr. med. univ.) and dentists (Dr. med. dent.), which do not technically possess doctorate degrees, but Diplom-level degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> A post-graduate research doctorate (Dr. med. univ. et sci. med.) can be obtained, for example, after three years post-graduate studies at a Faculty of Medicine.
</p><p>All doctors may be addressed as "Doktor ______", and the title is usually contracted to "Dr. ______". Contrary to popular belief, "Dr." is not part of the name but a degree like "Mag." or "Dipl.-Ing.". It is not mandatory to use the title, although it can be added to official documents (driver's licence, passport, etc.), if desired.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Finland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Finland, the title of <i>tohtori</i> or <i>doktor</i> denotes holder of a research doctorate comparable to a PhD. Getting the doctorate requires advanced studies after the master's degree, writing a <a href="/wiki/Thesis" title="Thesis">thesis</a>, nowadays often a <a href="/wiki/Thesis_by_publication" class="mw-redirect" title="Thesis by publication">thesis by publication</a>, and publicly defending the thesis. Customary doctorates do not exist even in the field of medicine: physicians hold the degree of <i>lääketieteen lisensiaatti</i> (<a href="/wiki/Licentiate_(degree)" title="Licentiate (degree)">Licentiate</a> of Medicine), and are referred to simply as <i>lääkäri</i> (physician); "tohtori" would be rustic or old-fashioned. A research doctorate in medicine, <i>lääketieteen tohtori</i>, requires further research work and a separate doctoral thesis, and is not taken by all medical graduates. Regardless, in Finnish usage, the use of titles is uncommon and restricted to only the most formal of contexts.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, the title of <i>Docteur</i> is only used generally for <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a> (<i>médecin</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Dentist" title="Dentist">dentists</a> (<i>chirurgiens-dentistes</i>) but can also be used by <a href="/wiki/Pharmacist" title="Pharmacist">pharmacists</a>, along with holders of research doctorates. Medical professionals do not normally hold a <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctorate</a>, which in France is used only to refer to a research doctorate, but a "State Diploma of Doctor of Medicine" (<i>Diplôme d'État de docteur en médecine</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The law in France allows the title of Dr to be used professionally by the above medical professionals and by holders of research doctorates, with the field of their speciality being mentioned after the name.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The courts have ruled that stating the specialisation is not necessary except in circumstances specifically related to professional practice; at other times the title alone may be used.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> The courts have also determined that questioning the right of the holder of a research doctorate to use the title Dr is a form of libel.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The National Union of Hospital Scientists (<i>Syndicat National des Scientifiques Hospitaliers</i>) launched a campaign in 2015 to raise awareness of the right of scientists to use the title.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German language</a>-speaking countries, the word <i>Doktor</i> refers to a doctorate awardee in formal language (similar to a PhD). It is distinct from <i>Arzt</i>, since a doctoral degree is not a requirement for medical practitioners, though colloquial use of the word <i>Doktor</i> for physician is common and ordinary people often incorrectly assume that only Doktors may practice medicine. For this reason, 80% of all students in medicine write "doctoral" dissertations, often comparable to a master's thesis in science,<sup id="cite_ref-Beisiegel791_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beisiegel791-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> alongside their undergraduate studies to obtain a Dr. med. degree. The <a href="/wiki/European_Research_Council" title="European Research Council">European Research Council</a> decided in 2010 that those Dr. med. doctorates do not meet the international standards of a PhD research degree.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the most common doctoral degrees are <i>Dr. med.</i> (medicine), <i>Dr. med. dent.</i> (dentistry), <i>Dr. med. vet.</i> (veterinary medicine), <i>Dr. rer. nat.</i> (natural sciences), <i>Dr. phil.</i> (humanities), <i>Dr. iur.</i> (law), <i>Dr. rer. pol.</i> (economic and political sciences, also as Dr. rer. oec. in Switzerland), <i><a href="/wiki/Doktoringenieur" title="Doktoringenieur">Dr.-Ing.</a></i> (engineering), and <i>Dr. theol.</i> (theology). All holders of doctoral degrees are appropriately addressed as "Herr/Frau Dr. _____" in all social situations.
</p><p>In Germany, double doctorates are indicated in the title by <i>"Dr. Dr."</i> or <i>"DDr."</i> and triple doctorates as <i>"Dr. Dr. Dr."</i> or <i>"DDDr."</i>. More doctorates are indicated by the addition of <i>"mult."</i>, such as <i>"Dr. mult."</i>. Honorary titles are shown with the addition of <i>"h.c."</i>, which stands for <i>"honoris causa"</i>. Example: <i>"Dr. h.c. mult."</i>
</p><p>All people holding a doctorate from an EU member state are, since 2001, entitled to use "Doctor" or "Dr." in all formal, legal and published communications without any further addenda. For academics with doctorates from non-EU member states, the qualification must be recognised formally ("validated") by the Federal Educational Ministry in Bonn. The recognition process can be done by the employer or employee and may be part of the official bureaucracy for confirming professional status and is dependent on individual bilateral agreements between Germany and other countries and, since 2007, the Lisbon Recognition Convention.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> An example of mutual recognition of Doctor titles among EU countries is the "Bonn Agreement of November 14, 1994", signed between Germany and Spain (prior to the general recognition of EU doctorates).<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, <a href="/wiki/Kultusministerkonferenz" title="Kultusministerkonferenz">The Standing Conference of the Ministers of Education and Cultural Affairs of the Länder in the Federal Republic of Germany</a> extended their 2001 decision to recognise EU PhDs to cover PhDs that were awarded in Australia, Israel, Japan, Canada, and some American universities. It was announced in 2012 that this would be further extended to cover PhDs awarded in New Zealand. PhDs that were awarded in the United States are recognized if the awarding institution is classified by the <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Foundation_for_the_Advancement_of_Teaching" title="Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching">Carnegie Foundation for the Advancement of Teaching</a> as a "Research University (high research activity)" or as a "Research University (very high research activity)." Permission to use the title covers only scientific research degrees and does not extend to professional degrees such as the M.D. or J.D.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
