<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greece">Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Greece, doctor "Διδάκτωρ" (<i>didaktōr</i>) is indicated in the title as Δρ and it is used for holders of doctoral degrees. Other alternative names are also used, namely «δόκτωρ» και «δόκτορας», derived from the French "docteur". The term "Ιατρός" (<i>iatros</i>) indicates medical practitioners.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hungary">Hungary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Hungary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Hungary, graduates of six-year medical schools (<i>dr. med.</i>), five-year dentistry schools (<i>dr. med. dent.</i>), five-year law schools (<i>dr. jur.</i>) and five-year veterinary medical schools (<i>dr. vet.</i>) receive the title of a <i>doctor</i> at the end of their studies, after completing and successfully defending their thesis; their undergraduate studies must have lasted for a minimum of three years. Completing a PhD research programme (or DLA in arts and music) also leads to the doctor title. Since 2008, also those who graduated of five-year <a href="/wiki/Pharmacy" title="Pharmacy">pharmacy</a> schools have the right to use the title "dr" (<i>dr. pharm.</i>).
</p><p>A large part of Hungarians with doctor titles received their titles for research in the old academic system before PhD was introduced in Hungary. Since the introduction of PhD title (1993), the <i>dr. univ</i> title (given before 1993). The CSc <i>Candidate of Science</i> title was a scientific title in the old academic system and is now treated as an equivalent of PhD. CSc titles were awarded by the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Hungarian Academy of Sciences">Hungarian Academy of Sciences</a>.
</p><p>The highest level doctoral degree in Hungary is DSc <i>Doctor of Sciences</i>, awarded by the Hungarian Academy of Sciences. It is also called <i>Doctor of the Hungarian Academy of Sciences</i>.
</p><p>The title of doctor used to become a part of the name and was added as such to personal identity documents. This practice is still common and graduates after receiving their diploma would usually change their personal documents in order to officially indicate the achievement.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Usage in Ireland is similar to that in the UK. The title of doctor is used for holders of doctoral degrees<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> as well as for medical practitioners (except surgeons),<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> dentists,<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> and vets.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The title is also used in Ireland for Catholic bishops, who are styled "The Most Reverend Dr X, Bishop of Y" on envelopes.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The first university of Western civilization, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a>, is located in Italy, where until modern times the only degree granted was that of the doctorate,<sup id="cite_ref-Catholic_Encyclopedia_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catholic_Encyclopedia-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> and all other Italian universities followed that model. During the 20th century Italian universities introduced more advanced research degrees, such as the Ph.D., and now that it is part of the E.U. <a href="/wiki/Bologna_Process" title="Bologna Process">Bologna Process</a>, a new three-year first degree, or "laurea" (equivalent to a B.A. of other countries, <a href="/wiki/European_Qualifications_Framework" title="European Qualifications Framework">EQF</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/ISCED" class="mw-redirect" title="ISCED">ISCED</a> 2011 Level 6), has been introduced. The old-style "laurea" is now known as "laurea magistrale/specialistica" (Bologna Process second cycle/<a href="/wiki/European_Qualifications_Framework" title="European Qualifications Framework">EQF</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/ISCED" class="mw-redirect" title="ISCED">ISCED</a> 2011 Level 7). For historical reasons, even to this day, the title of "dottore/dottoressa" (abbrev. both as dott/dott.ssa or as dr./dr.ssa ) is awarded even to those who have studied for a "laurea"(<a href="/wiki/European_Qualifications_Framework" title="European Qualifications Framework">EQF</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/ISCED" class="mw-redirect" title="ISCED">ISCED</a> 2011 Level 6). Upper levels of degree are anyway shown in the title, as those who obtain a master's degree can be referred to as "dottore/dottoressa magistrale" (<i>masterly doctor</i>, <a href="/wiki/European_Qualifications_Framework" title="European Qualifications Framework">EQF</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/ISCED" class="mw-redirect" title="ISCED">ISCED</a> 2011 Level 7) while those who achieve the relatively new program of "dottorato di ricerca" (research doctorate, equivalent of a <i>Ph.D.</i> in <a href="/wiki/English_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="English Language">English</a>-speaking or <i>Doktor</i> (<i>Dr.</i>) in <a href="/wiki/German_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="German Language">German</a>-speaking Countries, <a href="/wiki/European_Qualifications_Framework" title="European Qualifications Framework">EQF</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/ISCED" class="mw-redirect" title="ISCED">ISCED</a> 2011 Level 8), carry the title of "dottore/dottoressa di ricerca" (research doctor), which can be abbreviated as "Dott. Ric." or "Ph.D."<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malta">Malta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Malta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, the title of Doctor is used by academic doctors (with Ph.D.s), medical practitioners, dentists and lawyers. Its use by lawyers is due to the qualifying degree for practicing law having been the <a href="/wiki/Legum_Doctor" title="Legum Doctor">LLD</a> until reforms in 2014, and has been described as "historical baggage" by the Dean of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Malta" title="University of Malta">University of Malta</a>'s law school. Lawyers do not generally use the title when practicing outside of Malta.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Netherlands">The Netherlands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: The Netherlands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch language</a> the word "dokter" refers to a physician, whereas "doctor" refers to the academic title. The doctor title is abbreviated as dr. placed before the holders name (note the lowercase).
</p><p>To enter a Dutch doctoral defense, the candidate must hold a validated master's degree (a master's degree of an acknowledged university, or a master equivalent degree validated on a case by case basis by the Dutch government). In some cases the candidate can be granted special dispensation if no master's degree is held.
There is no specific notation of the discipline in which the doctorate is obtained. Once the doctorate is obtained the preceding master's degree is generally no longer reported. Exceptions only exist for the disciplines with specific master titles of engineering "ir." ("ingenieur", i.e. Engineer) and law "mr." ("meester", i.e. Master of Law) where the title dr. is added to the original master title. For these disciplines, the original master's degree abbreviation is combined with the dr. abbreviation thus resulting in for example "dr. ir. Familyman". The dr. title is always placed in front of the ir. title. In the case of a PhD in law, the original mr. title is placed before the dr. title (mr. dr., see e.g. <a href="/wiki/Jan_Peter_Balkenende" title="Jan Peter Balkenende">Jan Peter Balkenende</a>). For a person having a law master's degree, but holding a PhD in another field than law the mr. title is placed after the dr. title (dr. mr.). No specific notation or title for the medical disciplines exists in the Netherlands. Although a physician is usually referred to as "dokter" (note the spelling difference) this does not necessarily imply the physician holds a doctoral degree; nor does it give the physician a title equivalent to that of PhD.
</p><p>Confusion can be caused by the original Dutch Master level title "drs." (for all non-engineering and non-law master's degrees). This abbreviation stands for the Dutch title <a href="/wiki/Doctorandus" title="Doctorandus">doctorandus</a> Latin for "he who should become a doctor" (female form is "doctoranda"). Dutch drs. should not be confused with the plural 'doctorates': having a PhD in multiple disciplines. Once a doctorate is achieved the doctorandus is promoted to doctor, and no longer uses the drs. abbreviation.<br />
Stacking of multiples titles of the same level, as seen in countries like for example Germany (Dr. Dr. Dr. Musterfrau) is highly uncommon in the Netherlands (although stacking of titles with different levels is common: prof. dr. ir. Appelmans). Those who have multiple doctor titles may use dr.mult. before their name, although this is rarely used.
</p><p>After obtaining a doctorate successfully, Dutch doctors may bear either the title dr. (lower case) before, or the letter D (rarely in practice) behind their name, but not both simultaneously. In the Netherlands, Academic titles are used exclusively within academia. Holding a PhD title has become a standard requirement for a university career. The doctor title has little to no meaning or implications for public life outside academia. It cannot be added to documentation (e.g. passport, drivers licence), and is used infrequently in daily practice.
</p><p>Historically, The Netherlands used their own academic degree nomenclature, but for many years now have recognised and implemented the <a href="/wiki/Bologna_declaration" title="Bologna declaration">Bologna declaration</a>. In everyday practice, the Anglo Saxon titles (e.g. PhD) are frequently used. Dutch academic titles are legally protected. PhD degrees can only be granted by recognised (research) universities. Illegal use is considered a misdemeanor and subject to legal prosecution.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, up to recent times after the completion of an undergraduate degree – except in <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> – a person was referred to as <i>doutor (Dr.) – male</i> or <i>doutora (Dra.) – female</i>. The architects and engineers were referred by their professional titles: <i>arquitecto</i> (Arq.) and <i>engenheiro</i> (Eng.). <a href="/wiki/Nurse" class="mw-redirect" title="Nurse">Nurses</a> are also referred to as "nurse", <i>enfermeiro</i> (male) or <i>enfermeira</i> (female), the title being <i>Enf.</i> for both.
</p><p>Nowadays Portugal is a signatory to the <a href="/wiki/Bologna_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Bologna process">Bologna process</a> and according to the current legislation the title of Doctor (<i>doutor, doutora</i>) is reserved for graduate holders of an academic <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctorate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Professions such as <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Attorney_at_law" title="Attorney at law">attorneys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmacist" title="Pharmacist">pharmacists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veterinarian" title="Veterinarian">veterinarians</a>, and few others are usually referred to by the title Dr. (doutor) even if they have not been awarded a doctoral degree.
</p><p>However, custom gives the legislation little strength and most graduates use the Dr. title in its abbreviated form, although use of the full <i>Doutor</i> is normally restricted to those with doctorates.. Those who are both holders of an academic doctorate and Professors at a college level are generally referred to as Professor Doutor.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Spain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The social standing of Doctors in Spain is evidenced by the fact that only <a href="/wiki/Ph.D." class="mw-redirect" title="Ph.D.">Ph.D.</a> holders, <a href="/wiki/Grandees" class="mw-redirect" title="Grandees">Grandees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke" title="Duke">Dukes</a> can take seat and cover their heads in the presence of the King.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ph.D." class="mw-redirect" title="Ph.D.">Ph.D.</a> Degrees are regulated by Royal Decree (R.D. 1393/2007),<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> <i>Real Decreto</i> (in <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>). They are granted by the University on behalf of the King, and its Diploma has the force of a public document. The Ministry of Science keeps a national database of doctoral theses called TESEO.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Any person who uses the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish people">Spanish</a> title of <i>doctor/doctora</i> (or <i>Dr./Dra.</i>) without being included in this Government database can be prosecuted for fraud.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish Royal Academy">Spanish Royal Academy</a> recognises that it is used colloquially to describe medics, even without doctoral degrees, as well as (in the feminine form, <i>doctora</i>, abbreviated <i>Dra.</i>) the wives of doctors (i.e. holders of doctoral degrees) and medics, as well as "women who shine with wisdom and understanding".<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike other countries, until recently Spain has registered a comparatively small number of Doctor degree holders, but the tendency is changing.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> According to the Spanish Statistical Office (INE), less than 5% of M.Sc. degree holders are admitted to Ph.D. programs.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> This reinforces the prestige that Doctors have historically enjoyed in Spain's society.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Doctor is commonly used in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> as the title for a person who has received a doctoral degree or, as <a href="/wiki/Courtesy_titles_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Courtesy titles in the United Kingdom">courtesy title</a>, for a qualified medical practitioner or dentist who does not have a doctorate.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> There are no restrictions on the use of the title "Doctor" in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, except where, in commercial advertising, it might imply that the user holds a general medical qualification.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The UK government allows medical doctors and holders of doctorates to have the title recorded on the observations page of their UK passport.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> The lack of legal restrictions was confirmed in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a> in 1996 by health minister <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Malone" class="mw-redirect" title="Gerald Malone">Gerald Malone</a>, who noted that the title doctor had never been restricted by law to either medical practitioners or those with doctoral degrees in the UK, although the titles "physician, doctor of medicine, licentiate in medicine and surgery, bachelor of medicine, surgeon, general practitioner and apothecary" were protected.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the etiquette guide, <a href="/wiki/Debrett%27s" title="Debrett&#39;s">Debrett's</a>, holders of doctoral degrees and medical doctors (but not surgeons) should be addressed as "Doctor".<sup id="cite_ref-Debrett&#39;s_Academic_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debrett&#39;s_Academic-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Debrett&#39;s_Doctor_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debrett&#39;s_Doctor-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> For medical doctors, "Doctor" is a professional title rather than an academic one: it is due to their being a medical practitioner rather than their having gained a doctoral degree.<sup id="cite_ref-Debrett&#39;s_Medicine_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debrett&#39;s_Medicine-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Quality_Assurance_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Quality Assurance Agency">Quality Assurance Agency</a> states that "The use of the title 'Dr' by medical doctors is a historical abbreviation for the profession; it does not indicate a qualification at doctoral level".<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> On guest lists and seating plans for formal events, holders of academic doctorates (but not medical doctors or other people using the title as a courtesy title) are listed either as "Dr John Smith" or "John Smith, Esq, PhD", while untitled men (other than those holding doctorates) are shown as either "Mr John Smith" or "John Smith, Esq" (as appropriate to ensure the styling remains consistent).<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The title "Dr" is also used on <a href="/wiki/Visiting_card" title="Visiting card">visiting cards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Medical students in the UK normally complete a course of study leading to the degree of <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Medicine,_Bachelor_of_Surgery" title="Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery">Bachelor of Medicine and Bachelor of Surgery</a> (MBBS, BMBS or MBChB).<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The MD degree is not a qualifying degree in the UK, but can be either a  <a href="/wiki/Professional_doctorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Professional doctorate">professional doctorate</a>  (at the same academic level as a PhD), a doctorate by thesis, or a <a href="/wiki/Higher_doctorate" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher doctorate">higher doctorate</a>, depending on the university.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> To be eligible for an MD degree in the UK one must already hold an entry level medical degree (for example, MBBS, MBChB, BMed, or a North American MD degree) and usually must have had at least 5 years of postgraduate training and experience. Trainee doctors are permitted to use the title Doctor once they have started their post-graduation "<a href="/wiki/Medical_education_in_the_United_Kingdom#Continuing_medical_education" title="Medical education in the United Kingdom">Foundation Programme</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Debrett's states that medical doctors (except surgeons) should be addressed on envelopes as "Dr (full name), (medical qualifications)", e.g. "Dr John Smith, MD", "Dr Anne Jones, MB BS, FRCP", "Dr David Evans, MB ChB", contrary to the normal rule of not mixing titles and post-nominals. Surgeons (and dentists, if not holding a doctoral degree) should be "(full name), Esq, (medical/dental qualifications)",<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> e.g. "John Smith, Esq, MS, FRCS", "David Evans, Esq., BDS", but "Dr Anne Jones, DDS, FDS RCS",. By contrast, those holding (non medical or dental) doctoral degrees, if not surgeons, should be "Dr (full name)" without post-nominals on envelopes, e.g. "Dr John Smith".<sup id="cite_ref-Debrett&#39;s_Medicine_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debrett&#39;s_Medicine-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Debrett&#39;s_Academics_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debrett&#39;s_Academics-183">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/A_%26_C_Black" title="A &amp; C Black">A &amp; C Black</a>'s <i>Titles and Forms of Address</i> diverges from Debrett's on how to address envelopes to medical doctors, omitting the pre-nominal title of Dr (e.g. John Smith, Esq, MD; John Smith, MD; John Smith, MB)  except in Scotland and for <a href="/wiki/General_practitioner" title="General practitioner">general practitioners</a>, where the post-nominals are instead usually omitted (e.g. Dr John Smith). Black's also state that it is down to individual choice whether non-medical doctors are addressed on envelopes as "Dr John Smith" or "John Smith, Esq, PhD" (or appropriate letters for the doctorate held), with the exception of doctors of divinity, who would be "The Rev. J. Smith, DD" on the envelope and "Reverend Sir" in a formal salutation (informally in the salutation "Dear Dr Smith", and "Dr Smith" in speech).<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Black&#39;s_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black&#39;s-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The custom of not referring to surgeons (members and fellows of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Surgeons" title="Royal College of Surgeons">Royal College of Surgeons</a>) as Dr  has been commented on in the <i><a href="/wiki/British_Medical_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="British Medical Journal">British Medical Journal</a></i> and may stem from the historical origins of the profession such as that some barbers also used to function as surgeons.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, the then-president of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Surgeons" title="Royal College of Surgeons">Royal College of Surgeons</a> called upon surgeons to use the title of Dr, saying that the use of Mr or Miss was confusing to patients.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Black's note that <a href="/wiki/Gynaecologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Gynaecologist">gynaecologists</a> are addressed as surgeons in England and Wales but as doctors elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-Black&#39;s_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black&#39;s-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a similar manner to the medical MBBS, dentists qualify with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Dental_Surgery" class="mw-redirect" title="Bachelor of Dental Surgery">Bachelor of Dental Surgery</a> (BDS) and vets with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Veterinary_Science" title="Bachelor of Veterinary Science">Bachelor of Veterinary Science</a> (BVSc), <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Veterinary_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Bachelor of Veterinary Medicine">Bachelor of Veterinary Medicine</a> (BVetMed) or <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Veterinary_Science" title="Bachelor of Veterinary Science">Bachelor of Veterinary Medicine and Surgery</a> (BVMS). All of these are, like the MBBS, master's level qualifications that bear bachelor's designations for historical reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-FHEQ_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FHEQ-189">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dentists have traditionally (as dental surgeons) been referred to in the same way as surgeons, but since 1995 the <a href="/wiki/General_Dental_Council" title="General Dental Council">General Dental Council</a> have permitted dentists to use the title "Doctor",<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> though many do not choose to do this, thereby stressing their surgeon status. However, Debrett's continues to advises that dentists are normally addressed as surgeons  and that the title "Doctor" is usually only used for dentists who have a doctoral degree.<sup id="cite_ref-Debrett&#39;s_Medicine_173-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Debrett&#39;s_Medicine-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 5 March 2015 the council of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Veterinary_Surgeons" title="Royal College of Veterinary Surgeons">Royal College of Veterinary Surgeons</a> (RCVS) voted to permit its members to use the courtesy title of "Doctor". Guidance from the RCVS says the title should be used either with the description "veterinary surgeon"   or the postnominals "MRCVS" to ensure there is no confusion with doctors of human medicine or holders of doctoral degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Optometrist" class="mw-redirect" title="Optometrist">Optometrists</a> are not permitted to use the title "Doctor" based on their initial qualification (BOptom or BSc (Optom)).<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>  Optometrists can earn PhDs or <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Optometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Optometry">Doctor of Optometry</a> degrees (in the UK a PhD-level qualification for qualified optometrists with experience in practice<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>). <a href="/wiki/Ophthalmologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ophthalmologist">Ophthalmologists</a> are fully qualified medical doctors. However, ophthalmology is considered a branch of surgery, thus ophthalmologists, like other surgeons in the UK, do not use the title "Doctor".<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/General_Chiropractic_Council" title="General Chiropractic Council">General Chiropractic Council</a> permits registered chiropractors to use the title "Doctor", although it advises chiropractors to avoid using the title in advertising or, if they do, to spell out that they are "Doctors of Chiropractic".<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> The Committee of Advertising Practice advises, however, that "references to 'DC' or 'doctor of chiropractic' are unlikely to dispel that misleading impression [of being a medical practitioner], when used in conjunction with unqualified references to the prefix 'Dr<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>", saying that the use of the title by chiropractors may be acceptable in advertising if "clearly and prominently qualified with additional text which makes clear it is a courtesy title and that the practitioner does not hold a general medical qualification".<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Advertising_Standards_Authority_(United_Kingdom)" title="Advertising Standards Authority (United Kingdom)">Advertising Standards Authority</a> has ruled that practitioners of traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese medicine">Chinese medicine</a> should not use the title doctor in adverts unless they hold a general medical qualification and are registered with the <a href="/wiki/General_Medical_Council" title="General Medical Council">General Medical Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> It similarly advises that <a href="/wiki/Osteopath" class="mw-redirect" title="Osteopath">osteopaths</a> should not use the title unless holding a general medical qualification.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> There have also been rulings that an advert for an <a href="/wiki/Osteomyology" title="Osteomyology">osteomyologist</a> which referred to him as a doctor was misleading, as was an advert which used the title "Dr" and the post-nominals "PhD" on the basis of a PhD from an <a href="/wiki/Unaccredited_institutions_of_higher_education" title="Unaccredited institutions of higher education">unaccredited university</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Holders of honorary doctorates in the UK have the right, in most cases, to use the title of Doctor, although holders are encouraged to refrain from doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> Black's says that "The same rules apply as to other holders", although notes that the post-nominals would not normally be used.<sup id="cite_ref-Black&#39;s_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Black&#39;s-186">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some circumstances, "doctor" may be used on its own as a form of address rather than as a title before a name. This is limited to when the person is being addressed by their job title and so is only used for medical doctors.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Wales">Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Wales follows UK usage in English. In <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, the holder of a doctoral degree is <i>doethur</i> while a medical doctor is <i>doctor</i> or <i>meddyg</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The title "<i>y Doethur</i>" is used by those holding doctorates, e.g. "y Doethur Brinley Jones",<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>  "y Doethur John Elfed Jones,<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> which can be abbreviated "Dr".<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Medical doctors use, as in English, the title "Doctor", also abbreviated "Dr".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_Yugoslavia">Former Yugoslavia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Former Yugoslavia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In countries that were formerly <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavian</a> republics, such as <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> the title formally belongs to holders of academic doctoral degree such as <i><a href="/wiki/PhD" class="mw-redirect" title="PhD">PhD</a></i> or <a href="/wiki/Bologna_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Bologna process">pre-Bologna</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of science">dr.sc.</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of science">Doctor scientiae</a>) degree. The distinction is not formally accentuated (much like pre-Bologna <i><a href="/wiki/Master_of_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Master of science">mr.sc.</a></i> i.e. "<a href="/wiki/Master_of_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Master of science">Magister scientiae</a>" versus the Bologna <i>mag.</i>/<i>M.A.</i>/<i>M.Sc.</i>. which is informally often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Masters_degree" class="mw-redirect" title="Masters degree">masters</a>") between holders of two titles irrespective of the difference in years of study and required academic achievement which were both higher for earlier degrees. Informally it is not uncommon to accentuate that someone is holding a post-Bologna doctorate by using the PhD title, which was not used prior to Bologna, whereas holders of pre-Bologna degrees are common to use the earlier formal abbreviation of "dr.sc." that is not common in post-Bologna doctorates.
</p><p>Informally, the title, in both its full and abbreviated form (i.e. "dr.") is used honorifically to address medical doctors even in more formal environment like in lectored media texts, however officially it is reserved only for the holders of academic degrees. Formally the professional title for medical doctor is "lijekar" in <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montenegrin_language" title="Montenegrin language">Montenegrin</a>, "liječnik" in <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a>, "lekar/лекар" in <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_language" title="Macedonian language">Macedonian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> and "zdravnik" in <a href="/wiki/Slovenian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Slovenian language">Slovenian</a> and they can hold different academic degrees, as well as additional honorific title of <i>Primarius</i> that is given to distinguished <a href="/wiki/Specialty_(medicine)" title="Specialty (medicine)">specialists</a> with exemplary reputation and achievements.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>With the introduction of National Health Practitioner registration legislation on 1 July 2010, the title "doctor" is not restricted in any Australian state. The title "medical practitioner" is restricted for use by registered medical practitioners, while the title "doctor" is not restricted by law.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, the Medical Board of Australia advises that practitioners who are not medical practitioners who choose to use the title 'Doctor' (or 'Dr') should clearly state their profession in advertisements, even if they hold a PhD or another doctoral degree, e.g. 'Dr Smith (Dentist)' or 'Dr Jones (Chiropodist)'. The Psychology Board of Australia prohibits psychologists from using the title, to avoid confusion with psychiatrists, unless they hold a doctoral degree, in which case they must make it clear that they are not a medical practitioner or psychiatrist, e.g. by putting '(Psychologist)' after their name.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Australian_Qualifications_Framework" title="Australian Qualifications Framework">Australian Qualifications Framework</a> (AQF) defines doctoral degrees as being at Level 10 of the framework; it specifies that: "Individuals who have been awarded a Doctoral Degree at Level 10 on the AQF are entitled to use the title 'Doctor'. The title 'Doctor' will not be used by those who hold an honorary award."<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> The name 'Doctor' is also used in the name of some extended master's degrees at Level 9 (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Juris_Doctor" title="Juris Doctor">Juris Doctor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">Doctor of Medicine</a>); these are not considered doctoral degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbreviation">Abbreviation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Abbreviation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Doctor is abbreviated "Dr" in <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a> and "Dr." in <a href="/wiki/North_American_English" title="North American English">North American English</a>. The plural abbreviation is "Drs".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_usage">British usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: British usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In British English it is not necessary to indicate an abbreviation with a full stop after the abbreviation, when the first and last letters of the abbreviation are the same as for the unabbreviated word, i.e. the abbreviation is a contraction, as in the case for "<b>D</b>octo<b>r</b>".<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, it is normal also to omit stops from postnominal letters, thus the usual abbreviation for "Doctor of Philosophy" is "PhD" (or "DPhil", where this is used). The fully punctuated "Ph.D." or "D.Phil." is anachronistic and, where the abbreviated form of the degree is defined by regulation rather than custom (e.g. Oxford), may be technically incorrect.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_usage">American usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: American usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>American English makes no distinction between abbreviations that are contractions and abbreviations that are not. A period is used: the abbreviation of Doctor is usually written as "Dr." in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">US Postal Service</a> prefers punctuation to be omitted from addresses.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honorary_doctorates">Honorary doctorates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Honorary doctorates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">Honorary degree</a></div>
<p>An honorary doctorate is a doctoral degree awarded for service to the institution or the wider community.  It may also be awarded for outstanding achievement in a particular field.  This service or achievement does not need to be academic in nature. Often, the same set of degrees is used for higher doctorates, but they are distinguished as being <i>honoris causa</i>: in comprehensive lists, the lettering used to indicate the possession of a higher doctorate is often adjusted to indicate this, for example, "Hon. Sc.D.", as opposed to the earned research doctorate "<a href="/wiki/Sc.D." class="mw-redirect" title="Sc.D.">Sc.D.</a>". The degrees of Doctor of the University (D.Univ.) and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Humane_Letters" title="Doctor of Humane Letters">Doctor of Humane Letters</a> (D.H.L.), however, are only awarded as honorary degrees.
</p><p>By convention, recipients of honorary doctorates do not use the title "Dr" in general correspondence, although in formal correspondence from the university issuing the honorary degree it is normal to address the recipient by the title.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> However, this social convention is not always scrupulously observed; notable people who defied social convention and used the honorary prefix include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, who received honorary master's degrees from <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a> in 1753, and from <a href="/wiki/The_College_of_William_and_Mary" class="mw-redirect" title="The College of William and Mary">The College of William and Mary</a> in 1756,<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> and doctorates from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St._Andrews" class="mw-redirect" title="University of St. Andrews">University of St. Andrews</a> in 1759 and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> in 1762 for his scientific accomplishments.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> He thereafter referred to himself as "Doctor Franklin".<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maya_Angelou" title="Maya Angelou">Maya Angelou</a>, who held many honorary doctorates, called herself and was referred to by many as "Dr. Angelou" despite holding no undergraduate or advanced (non-honorary) degree.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Booker_T._Washington" title="Booker T. Washington">Booker T. Washington</a> was often referred to as "Dr. Washington" after receiving an honorary doctorate from <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sukarno" title="Sukarno">Sukarno</a>, President of <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, was awarded twenty-six honorary doctorates from various international universities including <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Berlin" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">Al-Azhar University</a>, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Belgrade" title="University of Belgrade">University of Belgrade</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lomonosov_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Lomonosov University">Lomonosov University</a> and many more. And also from domestic universities including the <a href="/wiki/Universitas_Gadjah_Mada" class="mw-redirect" title="Universitas Gadjah Mada">Universitas Gadjah Mada</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Universitas_Indonesia" class="mw-redirect" title="Universitas Indonesia">Universitas Indonesia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bandung_Institute_of_Technology" title="Bandung Institute of Technology">Bandung Institute of Technology</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Universitas_Padjadjaran" class="mw-redirect" title="Universitas Padjadjaran">Universitas Padjadjaran</a>. He had often been referred to by <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Indonesian Government">Indonesian Government</a> at the time as 'Dr. Ir. Sukarno',<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> combined with his degree in architecture (<a href="/wiki/Ingenieur" class="mw-redirect" title="Ingenieur">Ir.</a>) from <a href="/wiki/Bandung_Institute_of_Technology" title="Bandung Institute of Technology">Bandung Institute of Technology</a>. Sukarno is the president with the highest number of honorary doctorates in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Author and lexicographer <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a>, who had some years earlier been unable (due to financial considerations) to complete his undergraduate studies at <a href="/wiki/Pembroke_College,_Oxford" title="Pembroke College, Oxford">Pembroke College, Oxford</a>, was awarded the degree of Master of Arts by diploma in 1755, in recognition of his scholarly achievements.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>  In 1765, <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Dublin" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity College, Dublin">Trinity College, Dublin</a> awarded him the degree of <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Laws">Doctor of Laws</a><sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2-228">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> and in 1775 Oxford bestowed upon him the degree of <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Civil_Law" title="Doctor of Civil Law">Doctor of Civil Law</a> by diploma.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>  He never referred to himself as "Dr. Johnson", even though a degree by diploma is distinct from an honorary degree, but it was used by his contemporaries and in his biography by <a href="/wiki/James_Boswell" title="James Boswell">James Boswell</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson2_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson2-228">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span id="Other_uses_of_.22doctor.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses_of_&quot;doctor&quot;">Other uses of "doctor"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Other uses of &quot;doctor&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul><li>In some regions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">Southern United States</a>, "Doctor" is traditionally added to the first name of people holding doctorates, where it is used in either direct or indirect familiar address.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>"Doc" is a common <a href="/wiki/Doc_(nickname)" title="Doc (nickname)">nickname</a> for someone with a doctoral degree, in real life and in fiction — for example, the gunfighter <a href="/wiki/Doc_Holliday" title="Doc Holliday">Doc Holliday</a>, the Australian politician <a href="/wiki/H._V._Evatt" title="H. V. Evatt">H.V. "Doc" Evatt</a>, the character "Doc" in <i><a href="/wiki/Gunsmoke" title="Gunsmoke">Gunsmoke</a></i>, and pulp hero <a href="/wiki/Doc_Savage" title="Doc Savage">Doc Savage</a>.</li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a> and several other Christian denominations, a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a> is an eminent <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologian</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Thomas Aquinas">Saint Thomas Aquinas</a>, also known as the Angelic Doctor) from whose teachings the whole Church is held to have derived great advantage.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>African leaders often refer to themselves as "Doctor" as part of their title upon assuming office.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Doctor_(title)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-RCPHistory-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-RCPHistory_23-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RCPHistory_23-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Sir George Norman Clark; A. M. Cooke (1964). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=OQEpAQAAMAAJ&amp;q=%22and+address+by+%22"><i>A History of the Royal College of Physicians of London</i></a>. <b>3</b>. <a href="/wiki/Clarendon_Press" class="mw-redirect" title="Clarendon Press">Clarendon Press</a>. p.&#160;962. <q>In December 1838 a resolution had been passed that 'The College is prepared to regard in the same light, and address by the same appellation, all who have obtained its Diploma, whether they have graduated elsewhere or not', in other words, that all in the College without degrees were to be given the courtesy title of "Doctor". In 1859 the College changed its mind and resolved 'That the title of Doctor shall not be given in any official document issued from this College to any person who is not possessed of the Degree of Doctor of Medicine"</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+History+of+the+Royal+College+of+Physicians+of+London&amp;rft.pages=962&amp;rft.pub=Clarendon+Press&amp;rft.date=1964&amp;rft.au=Sir+George+Norman+Clark&amp;rft.au=A.+M.+Cooke&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DOQEpAQAAMAAJ%26q%3D%2522and%2Baddress%2Bby%2B%2522&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADoctor+%28title%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Robert Currie (1994). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=OP5ePl7i5EIC&amp;pg=PA125">"The Arts and Social Studies, 1914–1939"</a>. M. Walker, Senior Tutor of Queen's, proposed, as the <i>Oxford Magazine</i> put it, that the University 'should divert the stream' of American aspirants to the German universities' degree of <i>philosophiae doctor</i> by opening the DLitt to persons offering a suitable dissertation nine terms after graduation. Apart from a successful move led by Sidney Ball, philosophy tutor at St John's, to distinguish the proposed arrangement from both the DLitt and the German PhD by adopting the English title 'doctor of philosophy' (DPhil), the scheme meet with little opposition</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=The+Arts+and+Social+Studies%2C+1914%E2%80%931939&amp;rft.btitle=The+History+of+the+University+of+Oxford%3A+The+twentieth+century&amp;rft.pages=125&amp;rft.pub=Clarendon+Press&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.au=Robert+Currie&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DOP5ePl7i5EIC%26pg%3DPA125&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADoctor+%28title%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-cdqf-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-cdqf_77-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-cdqf_77-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.cmec.ca/Publications/Lists/Publications/Attachments/95/QA-Statement-2007.en.pdf">"Canadian Degree Qualifications Framework"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Ministerial Statement on Quality Assurance of Degree Education in Canada</i>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">5 December</span> 2016</span>. <q>Though considered to be bachelor's programs in academic standing, some  professional programs yield degrees with other nomenclature. Examples: DDS (Dental Surgery), MD (Medicine), LLB, or JD (Juris Doctor)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Ministerial+Statement+on+Quality+Assurance+of+Degree+Education+in+Canada&amp;rft.atitle=Canadian+Degree+Qualifications+Framework&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.cmec.ca%2FPublications%2FLists%2FPublications%2FAttachments%2F95%2FQA-Statement-2007.en.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADoctor+%28title%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-102">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://afterdeadline.blogs.nytimes.com/2010/04/13/faqs-on-style/">"FAQs on Style"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>. 14 November 2011. Who's a Dr.?<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">3 December</span> 2016</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.atitle=FAQs+on+Style&amp;rft.pages=Who%27s+a+Dr.%3F&amp;rft.date=2011-11-14&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fafterdeadline.blogs.nytimes.com%2F2010%2F04%2F13%2Ffaqs-on-style%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADoctor+%28title%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Paul Martin (15 June 2010). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=3mjxg9iNAQUC"><i>The Wall Street Journal Guide to Business Style and Us</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Simon_and_Schuster" class="mw-redirect" title="Simon and Schuster">Simon and Schuster</a>. pp.&#160;71–72. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781439122693" title="Special:BookSources/9781439122693"><bdi>9781439122693</bdi></a>. <q>The abbreviation <i>Dr.</i> should be used in second reference as a title before the names of those who are generally called doctor in their professions in the U.S. … If appropriate in context and if the individual desires it, <i>Dr.</i> is thus used after the first reference before the names of individuals who hold Ph.D.s and other doctoral degrees. Holders of Ph.D.s in the academic world, for example, often prefer to be called doctor. It usually isn't necessary to specify that the degree is a Ph.D., M.D. or other degree. But because the public tends to identify <i>Dr.</i> with physicians, if the individual's specialty isn't clear in context, mention it as quickly as possible. … Generally, try to establish the person's preference for using the title. Unless you know the person has a doctorate or other qualifying degree that he or she prefers to use, use <i>Mr., Miss, Mrs.</i> or <i>Ms.</i> If the individual's only doctorate is an honorary one, do not use <i>Dr.,</i> except in a quotation. Lawyers, despite their J.D. degrees, aren't called doctor. In general references to M.D.s, it is more precise to use <i>physicians</i> rather than <i>doctors.</i></q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Wall+Street+Journal+Guide+to+Business+Style+and+Us&amp;rft.pages=71-72&amp;rft.pub=Simon+and+Schuster&amp;rft.date=2010-06-15&amp;rft.isbn=9781439122693&amp;rft.au=Paul+Martin&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D3mjxg9iNAQUC&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADoctor+%28title%29" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
